Title: FinAgentBench: A Benchmark Dataset for Agentic Retrieval in Financial Question Answering
URL: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2508.14052
Published: N/A
Author: N/A
Highlights:
Accurate information retrieval (IR) is critical in the financial domain, where investors must identify relevant information from large collections of documents. Traditional IR methods—whether sparse or dense—often fall short in retrieval accuracy, as it requires not only capturing semantic similarity but also performing fine-grained reasoning over document structure and domain-specific knowledge. Recent advances in large language models (LLMs) have opened up new opportunities for retrieval with multi-step reasoning, where the model ranks passages through iterative reasoning about which information is most relevant to a given query. However, there exists no benchmark to evaluate such capabilities in the financial domain. To address this gap, we introduce FinAgentBench, the first large-scale benchmark for evaluating retrieval with multi-step reasoning in finance – a setting we term agentic retrieval. The benchmark consists of 26K expert-annotated examples on S&P-500 listed firms and assesses whether LLM agents can (1) identify the most relevant document type among candidates, and (2) pinpoint the key passage within the selected document. Our evaluation framework explicitly separates these two reasoning steps to address context limitations. This design enables to provide a quantitative basis for understanding retrieval-centric LLM behavior in finance. We evaluate a suite of state-of-the-art models and further demonstrated how targeted fine-tuning can significantly improve agentic retrieval performance. Our benchmark provides a foundation for studying retrieval-centric LLM behavior in complex, domain-specific tasks for finance.
[...]
To address this gap, we introduce the first large-scale benchmark, FinAgentBench, for evaluating generative retrieval systems in the financial domain, focusing on a setting we term agentic retrieval. Unlike prior benchmarks that assess retrieval in a single stage, our proposed benchmark evaluates the ability of LLM agents to reason and retrieve the relevant information through a two-stage pipeline: (1) identifying which document to retrieve, evaluated via document-level ranking, and (2) determining which part of the document to focus on, evaluated via chunk-level ranking. The benchmark contains about 26K samples and is constructed from real-world financial documents paired with expert-written queries and annotations, reflecting authentic use cases faced by professional investors. By capturing both retrieval accuracy and reasoning depth, it provides a foundation for systematically analyzing the strengths and limitations of LLM-based generative retrieval in high-stakes domains like finance.
[...]
using standard top- $k
[...]
Performance is measured using standard ranking metrics measure by top- $5$ results: normalized Discounted Cumulative Gain (nDCG), Mean Average Precision (MAP), and Mean Reciprocal Rank (MRR). For chunk-level evaluation, we report metrics over the top ranked passages, evaluated against expert-annotated graded relevance scores. All models operate in a retrieval-only setting without access to external tools or retrieval augmentation.
[...]

[...]

[...]

Fine-tuning yields substantial gains: for document-type ranking, nDCG improves from 0.758 to 0.808, and MRR increases from 0.872 to 0.933. Similar improvements are observed in chunk ranking, with MRR rising from 0.526 to 0.587. These results demonstrate that domain-specific supervision significantly enhances retrieval accuracy, both in selecting the correct document type and in pinpointing relevant information within the document. This highlights the importance of adapting LLMs to financial reasoning tasks through task-aligned training signals.

Title: Assessing RAG System Capabilities on Financial Documents
URL: https://aclanthology.org/2025.finnlp-2.9.pdf
Published: N/A
Author: N/A
Highlights:
Financial documents present unique challenges: they require factual extraction, quantitative reason ing with numerical data, and information synthesis across multiple documents for comprehensive re sponses. Evaluating RAG performance on these distinct task types requires specialized benchmarks that reflect real-world financial complexity. Existing Question-Answer (QA) datasets fo cus on Wikipedia articles (Yang et al., 2018, Kwiatkowski et al., 2019), academic papers (Pra manick et al., 2024), or single-domain sources (Pipitone and Alami, 2024, Ngo et al., 2024), and therefore fail to capture the heterogeneous nature of financial document collections. While finan cial QA benchmarks often focus on narrow regu latory domains, they neglect the broader spectrum of client-facing content. Moreover, no benchmark systematically evaluates the intersection of finan cial materials and the diverse types of tasks critical for the deployment of RAG. We introduce FinDoc-RAG, a QA benchmark comprising 600+ QA pairs from 46 documents in English from UBS AG and other UBS entities. Doc uments include product descriptions, investment guides, legal policies, and marketing materials with dense numerical content and regulatory references. The questions span nine complexity levels (L0-L8) that target factual extraction, quantitative reason ing, and multi-document synthesis. Evaluation of five RAG architectures –vector-based indexing, graph-enhanced RAG, hierarchical summary-style retrieval (e.g., Raptor 4.2), and Knowledge Graph (KG)– reveals systematic performance gaps: lead ing systems achieve 0.91 accuracy in factual ex traction but only 0.44 on multi-document synthesis tasks. Contributions: 1. We present FinDoc-RAG, a RAG-focused question-answer benchmark over heteroge neous financial documents. It comprises
[...]
This process is repeated to build a tree with multiple lev els of summaries. During retrieval, RAPTOR can either traverse the tree layer-by-layer or evaluate nodes across all layers to find the most relevant in formation. By capturing high- and low-level details about a text, this approach helps with handling a wide range of questions and improves the integra tion and relevance of the retrieved information. Graph RAG (GraphRAG) Edge et al. (2025) is a multistep method to answer questions from large text collections. First, it creates a graph-based in dex by building an entity knowledge graph and generating summaries for groups of related entities.
[...]
Following Luo et al. (202
[...]
exact text overlap
[...]
5.1 Benchmark complexity analysis Task type analysis reveals distinct performance pat terns across RAG architectures. Factual extraction tasks (L0, L1, L4, L5) have relatively stable per formance with accuracy ranging from 0.51 to 0.84
[...]
6 Conclusion Financial institutions increasingly rely on RAG sys tems for document processing, yet systematic eval uation on industry-specific content has remained limited. We assessed four state-of-the-art RAG ar chitectures using FinDoc-RAG, a benchmark com prising 600+ question-answer pairs from real finan cial documents across nine complexity levels tar geting factual extraction, information integration, and cross-document synthesis. Our evaluation reveals that architectural choice
[...]
impacts performance on different question types: while leading systems achieve 0.84 accuracy on ba sic extraction, performance drops substantially for complex synthesis tasks (0.31 average), with archi tectural differences amplifying at higher complex ity levels. Semi-structured approaches (RAPTOR) provide the most consistent performance across complexity levels, while knowledge graph augmen tation (GFM-RAG) excels at complex reasoning but shows variable baseline performance. Our anal ysis reveals that no single architecture dominates across all task types. These findings highlight the need of benchmarks like FinDoc-RAG for measuring progress toward reliable financial document understanding systems.

Title: FinRetrieval: A Benchmark for Financial Data Retrieval by AI Agents
URL: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2603.04403
Published: N/A
Author: Eric Y. Kim, Jie Huang
Highlights:
AI agents increasingly assist with financial research, yet no benchmark evaluates their ability to retrieve specific numeric values from structured databases. We introduce FinRetrieval, a benchmark of 500 financial retrieval questions with ground truth answers, agent responses from 14 configura tions across three frontier providers (Anthropic, OpenAI, Google), and complete tool call execution traces. Our evaluation reveals that tool availability dominates performance: Claude Opus achieves 90.8% accuracy with structured data APIs but only 19.8% with web search alone—a 71 percentage point gap that exceeds other providers by 3–4×. We find that reasoning mode benefits vary inversely with base capability (+9.0pp for OpenAI vs +2.8pp for Claude), explained by differences in base -mode tool utilization rather than reasoning ability. Geographic performance gaps (5.6pp US advantage) stem from fiscal year naming conventions, not model limitations. We release the dataset, evaluation code, and tool traces to enable research on financial AI systems.
[...]
We introduce FinRetrieval, a benchmark specifically designed to evaluate AI agents on financial data
[...]
retrieval. The benchmark comprises 500 questions targeting specific numeric values (e.g., “What was Apple’s 
Q3 2024 revenue?”), ground truth answers verified against source documents, and agent responses from 14
[...]
configurations spanning three frontier providers (Anthropic, OpenAI, Google
[...]
ating general -purpose agents. FinDER [9], FinAgentBench [10], and Finance Agent Benchmark [4] evaluate
[...]
retrieval over financial documents, with FinAgentBench introducing multi -step agentic reasoning and FAB providing tool access for SEC filing analysis. FAMMA [11] adds multilingual and multimodal dimensions. None of these benchmarks combine structured API access with general -purpose agents, and none release complete execution traces for behavioral analysis.
[...]
Retrieval-augmented generation. CRAG [15] provides comprehensive evaluation of RAG systems across
[...]
We evaluate 14 agent configurations across 500 financial retrieval questions, producing 7,000 total evalua tions. This section presents our main findings on accuracy, computational cost, tool availability impact, reasoning mode benefits, tool call efficiency, and geographic performance gaps.
[...]
5.3 Finding A: Tool Availability Impact 
The most dramatic finding concerns tool availability. When equipped with MCP tools providing structured 
access to financial data, Claude Opus achieves 90.8% accuracy (95% CI: 88.3–93.3%). Without these tools—
[...]
5.4 Finding B: Reasoning Benefits Vary by Model 
Extended reasoning provides varying benefits across models (Table 5). 
The root cause is not that reasoning helps OpenAI more, but that OpenAI’s base mode has weaker MCP 
tool utilization. Analysis of tool call traces reveals that OpenAI’s base mode makes an average of 1.03
[...]
discover_company_series (DS) calls per question, compared to 1.78 for Claude Opus—73% more exploration. 
OpenAI’s reasoning mode compensates for this gap through more careful series selection from MCP output, 
not through additional exploration (DS calls increase only to 1.18, a 15% gain).
[...]
We present FinRetrieval, a benchmark for evaluating AI agents on financial data retrieval. The dataset 
comprises 500 questions with ground truth answers, agent responses from 14 configurations across three 
frontier providers, and complete execution traces including all tool calls. 
Our evaluation reveals four key findings: 
1. Tool availability dominates performance. Claude Opus achieves 90.8% accuracy with structured
[...]
data tools but only 19.8% with web search alone—a 71pp gap that exceeds other models by 3–4×.
[...]
2. Reasoning benefits vary inversely with base capability. OpenAI gains +9.0pp from reasoning
[...]
4. Geographic gaps reflect data conventions. The 5.6pp US advantage for Claude stems from fiscal

Title: Peer review in NLP: resource papers - Hacking semantics
URL: https://hackingsemantics.xyz/2020/reviewing-data/
Published: 2020-04-16T11:54:24.000Z
Author: N/A
Highlights:
- Computational linguistic resources (lexicons, dictionaries, grammars): the value is in offering a detailed description of language from some point of view, as complete as possible.
[...]
- NLP tasks/benchmarks: the main argument is typically that the new benchmark is more challenging than previous ones. That claim obviously must be supported by experimental results.
[...]
Apples are apples, oranges are oranges, and both are good in their own way. It is pointless to reject a resource paper for not being a systems paper. To write a constructive review, first of all you need to see its contribution from the same methodological perspective as the authors. If there is a mismatch, if you’ve been assigned a paper with a type of contribution that is not in your research sphere, it is better to ask the AC to reassign it.
[...]
- Linguistic knowledge resources (grammars, dictionaries, lexical databases): knowledge of the rest of linguistic theory and all the other relevant resources. Ideally, you’ve built at least one resource of this type yourself.
[...]
- Corpora with linguistic annotation (syntax, anaphora, coreference, temporal relations): knowledge of the relevant linguistic theory and annotation experience, annotation reliability estimation, and existing resources in this particular subfield. Ideally, you’ve built at least one resource of this type yourself.
[...]
- Crowdsourced NLP training or testing datasets: knowledge of basic crowdsourcing methodology, awareness of potential problems such as artifacts (Gururangan et al., 2018) and annotator bias (Geva, Goldberg, & Berant, 2019), as well as other available datasets for this task. Ideally, you’ve built at least one resource of this type yourself.
[...]
Once you made sure that you are looking at the paper from the same methodological perspective as the authors, you need to actually judge its contribution. Of course, not every resource paper deserves to be published at a top NLP conference! The acceptance criteria are really not that different for systems and resources. Most conferences are interested in how novel is the approach, how substantial the contribution, how big the potential impact. The authors of an *ACL-worthy paper, of any type, do need to make a strong case on at least one of these counts.
[...]
High novelty: significant conceptual innovation Examples: new task, new annotation methodology; Counter-examples: using an existing framework to collect more data or update an existing resource, or simply translating an existing resource to another language.
[...]
High impact: addressing a widespread problem, presenting new methodology with high generalizability (across languages or tasks). Examples: discovering biases that affect multiple datasets, releasing time-sensitive data (e.g. the recent dataset of research papers on coronavirus); Counter-examples: mitigating a specific bias induced by annotator guidelines in one specific dataset.
[...]
High quality, richness, or size: significant public data releases that offer clear advantages in the depth of linguistic description, data quality, or volume of the resource. Examples: linguistic databases like VerbNet, corpora with linguistic annotation, data collected organically in specific contexts (such as anonymized medical data); Counter-examples: noisy data without clear advantages over alternatives, non-openly-available data.
[...]
To reiterate: a paper could be publication-worthy by meeting only one of these criteria. A narrow problem can be solved in a highly novel way. A noisy dataset can have high impact if it’s all there is. A resource simply recast to another language might make big waves if it shows that the techniques developed for the English version completely fail to generalize. But the authors do need to show that at least one criterion applies strongly, and convince the reviewers that there are no serious flaws (e.g. if the inter-annotator agreement was inflated by discarding large portions of data).

---

We worked as a team to ensure that all submitted papers received at least three reviews and a meta-review, which required rounds of reminder emails and invitation of emergency reviewers. In keeping with the practices observed in EMNLP 2024, we included the following best paper awards 
categories: 
• Best Papers (featuring < 0.25% of accepted papers) and Outstanding Papers (featuring < 2.5% 
of accepted papers) present fascinating, controversial, surprising, impressive, and/or potentially 
field-changing ideas.

Title: Call for Main Conference Papers - EMNLP 2026
URL: https://2026.emnlp.org/calls/main_conference_papers/
Published: N/A
Author: N/A
Highlights:
EMNLP 2026 invites the submission of long and short papers featuring substantial, original, and unpublished research on empirical methods for Natural Language Processing. EMNLP 2026 has a goal of curating a diverse technical program—in addition to traditional research results, papers may contribute negative findings, survey an area, announce the creation of a new resource, argue a position, report novel linguistic insights derived using existing computational techniques, and reproduce (or fail to reproduce) previous results. As in recent years, some of the presentations at the conference will be of papers accepted by the Transactions of the ACL (TACL) and the Computational Linguistics (CL) journals.
[...]
All authors are expected to sign up to review, or serve as an Area Chair or Senior Area Chair, with assignments
[...]
based on qualifications.
[...]
submission, all authors must
[...]
6 EoD AoE. If you receive assignments, your reviews must be completed by July
[...]
your meta-reviews - by July
[...]
emergencies, the chairs should be warned
[...]
the emergency declaration form. Exemptions to this policy are detailed in the
[...]
Large language models have rapidly shifted from research prototypes to widely used infrastructure. This new reality is changing the center of gravity of NLP research, and progress can no longer be measured only by fractional improvements in benchmark scores. When strong general-purpose models are increasingly available through open releases and commercial platforms, the field faces a more foundational question: What are the missions of NLP research now, and what kinds of contributions most advance that mission?
[...]
The EMNLP 2026 special theme invites papers that articulate, test, and advance visions for NLP in this era. We welcome work that reflects on the mission(s) of NLP research, reframes our collective research goals, proposes new evaluation and scientific methodologies, and builds bridges across multiple disciplines that can sharpen both our theories and our impact. Submissions may be empirical, theoretical, or position and survey papers, but should be grounded in clear claims, strong evidence, and actionable insights.
[...]
This special theme is intended to complement the breadth of EMNLP, not narrow it. We encourage submissions that are ambitious in scope while remaining concrete in claims and methodology, and that help the community clarify what research looks like in the next chapter of NLP.
[...]
EMNLP 2026 will use ACL Rolling Review(ARR) as a reviewing system, but final decisions will be made by the conference. Both submissions of articles for review and commitment of reviewed articles to the conference will be performed via the Open Review platform. Specifically, authors will follow a two-step process:
[...]
- Authors submit articles to ARR, where submissions receive reviews and meta-reviews from ARR reviewers and action editors;
- Authors commit their reviewed articles to a publication venue (e.g., EMNLP 2026), where Senior Area Chairs and Program Chairs make acceptance decisions from the ARR reviews and meta-reviews.
[...]
Both long and short paper submissions should follow all of the ARR submission requirements, including:
[...]
Final versions of accepted papers will be given one additional page of content (up to 9 pages for long papers, up to 5 pages for short papers) to address reviewers’ comments.
[...]
All accepted papers must be presented at the conference to appear in the proceedings. The conference will include both in-person and virtual presentation options. Papers without at least one presenting author registered by the early registration deadline may be subject to rejection. Long and short papers will be presented orally or as posters as determined by the program committee. While short papers will be distinguished from long papers in the proceedings, there will be no distinction in the proceedings between papers presented orally and papers presented as posters.

For background on the checklist and guidance provided to the authors, see the Responsible NLP Checklist
page at ACL Rolling Review.

[...]

Title: FAQ - EMNLP 2026
URL: https://2026.emnlp.org/faq/
Published: N/A
Author: N/A
Highlights:
FAQ - EMNLP 2026
[...]
While we spent a considerable amount of time going over outliers cases (e.g. where the difference of avg. reviewer score to the AC score was large), ultimately, we had to rely on the multiple layers of the review process and usually deferred to the judgement of the SACs who had access to a much larger pool of papers and reviews than individual reviewers and ACs. The SACs were hand-picked for papers in specific tracks both because of their experience with the review process, as well as their publication record in our field and in the relevant area. We do not take it upon ourselves to second-guess all of the work the SACs do. We may not fully agree, but we only overturn in cases that diverge substantially from expected levels variation across levels in the process.

---

Title: Criteria for Determining Irresponsible Reviewers - EMNLP 2025
URL: https://2025.emnlp.org/reviewer-policies/
Published: 2025-05-06T02:49:24.000Z
Author: N/A
Highlights:
Criteria for Determining Irresponsible Reviewers - EMNLP 2025
[...]
This post accompanies the ARR post Changes to reviewer volunteering requirement and incentives, and defines the specific criteria for what we will deem as “Highly Irresponsible” reviewers. While the focus here is reviewers, we will use similar criteria for determining irresponsible Area chairs (ACs).
[...]
If a reviewer fails to submit their reviews by the official deadline and has not submitted a personal emergency declaration(note: declaring a personal emergency after the review deadline will not be considered) will automatically be flagged as “Highly Irresponsible”.
[...]
### 2. Extremely terse or unprofessional reviews
[...]
Where the submissions are good-faith work that merits a serious review (otherwise a short review can suffice, assuming it clearly explains the fundamental problems with that work), reviews that only contain a single argument (1-2 sentences) and no constructive feedback should be flagged. We may also penalize reviews that are extremely unprofessional in tone (e.g., rude, racist, sexist, ableist, etc. content; I4 in the list of 12 commonly reported issues), even if they are otherwise detailed.
[...]
Here are some guidelines for determining whether to consider a submission to be in good faith: At minimum, a good faith article states the contribution up front and provides an evaluation that supports that. If the writing is so poor that the intended contribution can’t be identified or the article is missing an evaluation positioned as supporting that, then the article does not warrant a serious review. If the issue is just that the stated contribution is not clear, or the evaluation is not sufficient or rigorous enough, that does warrant a serious review. Furthermore, if the paper shows a naivete about the state of the art, the paper still warrants a serious review, but if the paper shows a complete lack of awareness of work in the field (for example, if virtually all of the citations are from another field), then the paper is not a good faith submission. Even interdisciplinary papers should show an awareness of the audience they are submitting their work to.
[...]
As per the ACL Policy on Publication Ethics, it is acceptable to use LLMs for paraphrasing, grammatical checks and proof-reading, but not for the content of the (meta-)reviews. Furthermore, the content of both the submission and (meta-)review is confidential. Therefore, even for acceptable purposes such as proofreading, it must not be passed on to non-privacy-preserving third parties, such as commercial LLM services, which may store it.
[...]
Authors will be able to flag such cases and present any evidence they have to support their allegation. While there is no definitive way of determining whether a review was (entirely) generated by an LLM, the Program Chairs will review the evidence and only proceed in cases where there is no reasonable doubt.
[...]
The process is specified in the ARR post. Ultimately, all decisions will be made by the Program Chairs after a careful review of all evidence. Reviewers/ACs will be able to appeal to the publication ethics committee1 if they want to dispute the Program Chairs decisions.

---

Title: Call for Main Conference Papers - EMNLP 2025
URL: https://2025.emnlp.org/calls/main_conference_papers/
Published: N/A
Author: N/A
Highlights:
EMNLP 2025 invites the submission of long and short papers featuring substantial, original, and unpublished research on empirical methods for Natural Language Processing. EMNLP 2025 has a goal of curating a diverse technical program—in addition to traditional research results, papers may contribute negative findings, survey an area, announce the creation of a new resource, argue a position, report novel linguistic insights derived using existing computational techniques, and reproduce, or fail to reproduce, previous results. As in recent years, some of the presentations at the conference will be of papers accepted by the Transactions of the ACL (TACL) and the Computational Linguistics (CL) journals.
[...]
At the time of submission to ARR, authors will be asked to select a preferred venue (e.g., EMNLP 2025). This is used only to calculate acceptance rates. Authors who selected EMNLP 2025 as a preferred venue when submitting to ARR may choose not to commit to EMNLP 2025 after receiving their reviews, and authors who selected a preferred venue other than EMNLP 2025 when submitting to ARR are still welcome to commit to EMNLP 2025.
[...]
ACL adopted a policy similar to CVPR 2025 policy. All authors are expected to sign up to review, with assignments
[...]
based on qualifications. The highly irresponsible reviewers may become ineligible from committing their paper(s) to EMNLP or resubmitting in the next cycle. The submitting authors should (a) make sure that all other authors are aware of this policy, and (b) check that everybody on their team(s) submits their (meta-)reviews on time and in accordance with the guidelines. After submission, all authors must complete the author registration form by May
[...]
025 EoD AoE. If they get assignments, reviews must be completed by June 18, and meta-reviews - by July
[...]
15. In case of any emergencies, the chairs should be warned via the emergency declaration form. More details on the policy here: https://acl
[...]
review.org/incentives
[...]
swering
- Resources and Evaluation
- Semantics: Lexical, Sentence-level Semantics, Textual Inference and Other areas
[...]
Over the past two decades, the field has advanced at an exponential rate. The term language models is now a household word, industry is booming, and the publication rate is dizzying, but what does that mean about fundamental scientific impact and broader impact on real societal problems? How can we measure that in a rigorous way? Scores on benchmarks are increasing, however, to what extent do our benchmarks reflect the true impact of our technology advances? If we make a distinction between impact within our own field versus impact from our field into other fields, would we see the same magnitude of growth? The conventional measures of success don’t facilitate making critical distinctions, like incremental improvement versus transformative change, or within-field uptake versus broad impact across fields.
[...]
researchers with leaders from
[...]
community in reflection related to the theme
[...]
EMNLP 2025 will use ACL Rolling Review(ARR) as a reviewing system, but final decisions will be made by the conference. Both submissions of articles for review and commitment of reviewed articles to the conference will be performed via the Open Review platform. Specifically, authors will follow a two-step process:
[...]
- Authors submit articles to ARR, where submissions receive reviews and meta-reviews from ARR reviewers and action editors;
- Authors commit their reviewed articles to a publication venue (e.g., EMNLP 2025), where Senior Area Chairs and Program Chairs make acceptance decisions from the ARR reviews and meta-reviews.
[...]
Both long and short paper submissions should follow all of the ARR submission requirements, including:
[...]
Final versions of accepted papers will be given one additional page of content (up to 9 pages for long papers, up to 5 pages for short papers) to address reviewers’ comments.

Limitations Section One innovation of EMNLP 2022 is the requirement that each submitted paper must include an explicitly named Limitations section, discussing the limitations of the work. This discus sion does not count towards the page limit, and we asked reviewers to not use the mentioned limitations as reasons to reject the paper, unless there is a really good reason to. The effect was overall positive: most papers included a limitations section, and many of them were informative. We hope to see this requirement continue in the future conferences.

**Input:**
```json
{
  "filePath": "/home/tanmay/EMNLP2026/finBharat_plan_updated.md",
  "content": "# FinBharat — Updated Benchmark & Paper Plan for EMNLP 2026\n\n> **Updated:** May 2026 | **Target:** EMNLP 2026 (ARR May 2026 cycle, commitment phase)\n> **Original plan reviewed:** `finBharat_plan.md`\n\n---\n\n## 0. Executive Summary of Changes\n\nThe original plan is solid in scope, task definition, and metric design. However, after analyzing the actual dataset and surveying the 2025–2026 financial NLP landscape (PHANTOM, FAITH, FinLongDocQA, ARQA, FABRIC, BhashaBench-Finance, FinGround, MULTIFINBEN, etc.), the following gaps must be addressed for EMNLP acceptance:\n\n| # | Issue | Severity |\n|---|-------|----------|\n| 1 | **No human validation** of LLM-generated QA pairs — biggest reviewer concern | Critical |\n| 2 | **No multilingual / Indic-language coverage** — missed differentiator for India-focused benchmark | Critical |\n| 3 | **No RAG pipeline evaluation** — all existing 2025–26 financial benchmarks include RAG tracks | High |\n| 4 | **Model panel is vague** — \"family A-7B\" placeholders; needs concrete, current model names | High |\n| 5 | **No cross-benchmark comparison** — reviewers will ask \"how does this compare to FinQA / PHANTOM / FAITH?\" | High |\n| 6 | **Dataset quality analysis missing** — no inter-annotator agreement, no answerability audit, no evidence-grounding verification | High |\n| 7 | **Missing BRSR/ESG-specific evaluation track** — the data is rich in BRSR content but the plan treats it as generic \"section\" analysis | Medium |\n| 8 | **No statistical significance testing** | Medium |\n| 9 | **Paper framing too narrow** — \"hallucination benchmark\" is not the strongest hook; need India-specific + table-heavy + BRSR narrative | Medium |\n| 10 | **Missing Limitations and Ethics sections** — desk-reject risk at EMNLP | Medium |\n| 11 | **No leaderboard / HuggingFace release plan** — expected for benchmark papers in 2026 | Medium |\n| 12 | **No conversational / multi-turn QA** — ConvFinQA-style extension | Low-Medium |\n\nEach issue is addressed in the sections below.\n\n---\n\n## 1. Revised High-Level Goal & Positioning\n\n### 1.1 Rename to FinBharat\n\n\"FinAR-Hall\" is generic. **FinBharat** signals:\n- **India-focused** (Bharat = India in Hindi/Sanskrit)\n- **Financial** domain\n- Covers both **QA accuracy** and **hallucination/faithfulness**\n- Echos naming convention of recent benchmarks (FinBen, FinMTEB, BhashaBench-Finance, FABRIC)\n\n### 1.2 Revised Contribution Bullets\n\n1. **FinBharat**: the first large-scale, India-focused financial QA and hallucination benchmark built from 187 annual reports (BSE/NSE-listed companies) across 187 sectors, with **84K+ QA pairs** across 4 difficulty tiers, structured evidence links, and rich BRSR/ESG content.\n\n2. **Multi-dimensional evaluation suite**: numeric correctness (Num-Exact/Num-F1/MAPE), evidence grounding, NLI-based faithfulness, LLM-judge faithfulness with hallucination type taxonomy, and BRSR-specific compliance metrics — all with **human-validated gold subsets**.\n\n3. **RAG evaluation track**: the first Indian financial benchmark to include a full RAG pipeline evaluation (retrieval → generation → faithfulness) with multiple retrieval architectures.\n\n4. **Comprehensive model evaluation**: 12+ models (open + closed), with cross-benchmark comparisons to FinQA, TAT-QA, and PHANTOM, plus a detailed error taxonomy and sector-wise analysis.\n\n5. **Bilingual extension (English + Hindi)**: a translated subset demonstrating cross-lingual performance gaps for Indian financial QA.\n\n### 1.3 Key Differentiators from Existing Benchmarks\n\n| Feature | FinQA | TAT-QA | PHANTOM | FAITH | FinLongDocQA | ARQA | FABRIC | **FinBharat** |\n|---------|-------|--------|---------|-------|--------------|------|--------|---------------|\n| India-focused annual reports | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✓ (advice) | ✓ |\n| BRSR/ESG content | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✓ |\n| 4 difficulty tiers | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✓ (3) | ✗ | ✓ |\n| Full RAG pipeline eval | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | Partial | ✗ | ✗ | ✓ |\n| Hallucination detection | ✗ | ✗ | ✓ | ✓ | ✗ | ✗ | ✓ | ✓ |\n| Forensic/hard QA | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✓ |\n| Multilingual (Indic) | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✓ (6 lang) | ✓ (EN+HI) |\n| Cross-benchmark comparison | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✓ |\n| Human-validated subset | ✓ | ✓ | ✓ | ✓ | ✓ | ✓ | ✓ | ✓ |\n| 187 sectors | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✗ | ✓ |\n\n---\n\n## 2. Dataset: Consolidated Statistics & Quality Assurance\n\n### 2.1 Actual Dataset Statistics (from analysis)\n\n| Metric | Count |\n|--------|-------|\n| Sectors | 187 |\n| Companies | 187 (1 per sector, FY2025) |\n| Chunks | ~182,000 (across h_m + e_m) |\n| Semantic bundles | ~22,700 |\n| Easy QA pairs | ~21,660 |\n| Medium QA pairs | ~21,340 |\n| Hard QA pairs | ~19,940 |\n| Multihop QA pairs | ~21,350 |\n| **Total QA pairs** | **~84,300** |\n| Question types | Text Only, Table Only, Table with Text, Numerical Calculation |\n\n### 2.2 NEW: Human Validation (Critical Addition)\n\n**Problem:** All QA pairs are LLM-generated. Reviewers at EMNLP will flag this as a major weakness without human validation.\n\n**Solution:**\n\n1. **Stratified sampling for human validation:**\n   - Sample 500 QA pairs (125 per difficulty tier), stratified by question type and sector.\n   - Recruit 3 domain experts (CAs / finance professionals / academic researchers) for annotation.\n   \n2. **Human validation tasks:**\n   - **Answer correctness:** Is the gold answer actually correct given the evidence? (Yes/No/Partially)\n   - **Evidence sufficiency:** Does the evidence fully support the answer? (Yes/No)\n   - **Answerability:** Is the question answerable from the provided evidence? (Yes/No)\n   - **Hallucination labeling:** For model-generated answers, label as {faithful, intrinsic hallucination, extrinsic hallucination} with subtype flags.\n   \n3. **Inter-annotator agreement:**\n   - Report Fleiss' κ for correctness and Cohen's κ for pairwise.\n   - Target κ > 0.7. If below, resolve via adjudication and report both raw and adjudicated scores.\n\n4. **Dataset quality report (Section 3 of paper):**\n   - Gold answer accuracy rate (% correct after human review).\n   - Evidence sufficiency rate.\n   - Error types in LLM-generated QA (fabrication, wrong extraction, misattribution, etc.).\n   - Revision protocol: correct or discard flagged items.\n\n### 2.3 NEW: Evidence Grounding Audit\n\n- For 1,000 random QA pairs, compute:\n  - **Evidence traceability score** (already in plan) — but also report aggregate statistics.\n  - **Number hallucination rate**: fraction of numbers in gold answers NOT found in evidence text.\n  - **Table grounding rate**: for Table/Table-with-Text QAs, what fraction of answer numbers can be traced to specific table cells?\n  \n- Report these as **intrinsic dataset quality metrics** in the paper. This demonstrates rigor that reviewers expect.\n\n### 2.4 Data Split (Revised)\n\n- **Test:** 20% of reports (38 companies) — held out, never used in any development.\n- **Dev:** 20% of reports (38 companies).\n- **Train:** 60% of reports (111 companies) — optional for fine-tuning experiments.\n\n**Stratification constraints:**\n- Each split must contain: ≥5 companies with BRSR-heavy content, ≥3 financial-sector companies, ≥3 manufacturing companies, ≥3 services companies.\n- Ensure all question types and difficulty tiers are represented in each split.\n\n---\n\n## 3. Tasks (Revised & Extended)\n\n### 3.1 Task 1: Core QA (FinBharat-QA) — Same as original\n\nNo changes needed. Metrics are well-defined.\n\n### 3.2 Task 2: Faithfulness & Hallucination (FinBharat-Hall) — Enhanced\n\n**Additions:**\n\n1. **Hallucination type taxonomy** (aligned with PHANTOM and FinGround):\n   - **Intrinsic hallucination:** Answer contradicts evidence.\n     - Numeric fabrication (number not in evidence)\n     - Numeric miscalculation (arithmetic error)\n     - Misattribution (wrong row/year/segment)\n   - **Extrinsic hallucination:** Answer includes information not in evidence.\n     - Over-interpretation / speculation\n     - External knowledge injection\n   - **Cherry-picking:** Selective evidence usage that distorts meaning.\n\n2. **Atomic claim decomposition** (inspired by FinGround):\n   - Decompose each model answer into atomic claims.\n   - Verify each claim against evidence.\n   - Report claim-level faithfulness (fraction of entailed claims).\n\n3. **Human-labeled hallucination detection test set:**\n   - For 500 QA pairs, generate answers from 3 models.\n   - Human-annotate 1,500 (answer, evidence) pairs for faithfulness.\n   - Use this as gold standard for hallucination detection evaluation.\n   - Report accuracy, precision, recall, F1 of NLI and LLM-judge against human labels.\n\n### 3.3 Task 3: Evidence Quality & Retrieval — Same as original\n\nNo changes needed.\n\n### 3.4 NEW: Task 4 — RAG Pipeline Evaluation (FinBharat-RAG)\n\n**Motivation:** Every major 2025–26 financial benchmark (FinLongDocQA, FinAgentBench, FinDoc-RAG, OmniEval) includes RAG evaluation. Without this track, the paper will look outdated.\n\n**Task definition:**\n\nGiven `(question, full document chunks)`, execute a RAG pipeline: retrieve → generate → verify.\n\n**Pipelines to evaluate:**\n\n1. **Dense retrieval + LLM:** BGE-base / GTE-Qwen2-1.5B + LLM generator.\n2. **Hybrid retrieval (BM25 + dense) + LLM:** Standard hybrid with reciprocal rank fusion.\n3. **Raptor-style hierarchical retrieval + LLM:** Cluster-based summary retrieval.\n4. **Graph-RAG:** Entity-graph enhanced retrieval.\n5. **Agentic RAG (multi-round):** Iterative retrieve-reason-verify loop (inspired by FinLongDocAgent).\n\n**Metrics:**\n\n- Retrieval: Recall@k, MRR@k, nDCG@k (chunk level).\n- End-to-end QA: EM, Num-Exact, BERTScore.\n- Faithfulness: NLI entailment ratio + LLM-judge score on RAG-generated answers.\n- Latency and cost per query.\n\n**Key comparison:** Gold-bundle context vs. retrieved context — show the retrieval gap.\n\n### 3.5 Task 5: Numeric & Table QA (FinBharat-Numeric) — Enhanced\n\n**Additions:**\n\n1. **Operation-type breakdown** (aligned with FAITH taxonomy):\n   - Direct Lookup\n   - Comparative Calculation (YoY, QoQ)\n   - Bivariate Calculation (ratios, margins)\n   - Multivariate Calculation (complex derivations)\n\n2. **MAPE (Mean Absolute Percentage Error):**\n   - For numeric answers, report MAPE alongside Num-Exact and Num-F1.\n   - Also report **tolerance-based accuracy** (±1%, ±5%, ±10%) as in FinLongDocQA.\n\n3. **Table structure analysis:**\n   - Performance on simple tables vs. hierarchical tables vs. multi-row/multi-column tables.\n   - Impact of oversized tables (flagged in `oversized_table` field).\n\n### 3.6 Task 6: Multi-hop Reasoning (FinBharat-MultiHop) — Same as original\n\nNo changes needed. The hop-count analysis and support coverage metrics are good.\n\n### 3.7 Task 7: Hard Forensic QA (FinBharat-Hard) — Enhanced\n\n**Additions:**\n\n1. **Red-flag detection as binary classification:**\n   - Label each hard QA as \"flags an issue\" vs. \"confirms compliance.\"\n   - Evaluate models on their ability to identify red flags (precision, recall for issue detection).\n\n2. **Governance risk scoring:**\n   - For hard QAs in governance/auditor sections, create a composite governance risk score.\n   - Correlate model performance with sector-level risk.\n\n### 3.8 NEW: Task 8 — BRSR/ESG-Specific Evaluation (FinBharat-ESG)\n\n**Motivation:** Your dataset is uniquely rich in BRSR content (Business Responsibility and Sustainability Report) — this is the India-specific differentiator. No existing financial benchmark evaluates ESG/BRSR QA.\n\n**Task definition:**\n\n- Filter QAs from BRSR sections (sections containing \"BRSR\", \"Business Responsibility\", \"Sustainability\", \"ESG\", \"Principle 1–9\").\n- Evaluate separately to show how models handle ESG-specific questions.\n\n**Metrics:**\n\n- Standard QA metrics (EM, F1, Num-Exact) on BRSR subset.\n- **KPI extraction accuracy:** For BRSR Core KPIs (emissions, water, energy, gender ratios, etc.), measure exact match of numeric values.\n- **Compliance completeness:** What fraction of mandatory BRSR disclosures can the model correctly extract?\n- **Greenwashing detection:** For hard QAs in BRSR sections, can models identify vague/unsupported ESG claims vs. specific, quantified claims?\n\n### 3.9 NEW: Task 9 — Bilingual Evaluation (English + Hindi)\n\n**Motivation:** FABRIC and BhashaBench-Finance demonstrate that Indian financial benchmarks must include Indic languages. FABRIC shows Indian models don't perform better on Indian finance in English — and there's a 5–7% drop from English to Hindi. This is a compelling finding.\n\n**Design:**\n\n1. Translate 2,000 QA pairs (500 per difficulty tier) from English to Hindi using:\n   - Professional translators (preferred, for 500 pairs).\n   - Google Translate / IndicTrans2 + human post-edit (for remaining 1,500).\n   \n2. Translate corresponding evidence chunks to Hindi.\n\n3. Evaluate same model panel on Hindi QA and report:\n   - Performance gap: English vs. Hindi.\n   - Whether Indian-origin models (e.g., Sarvam, Navarasa) perform better on Hindi.\n   - Whether code-switched (Hinglish) prompts help (as FABRIC found).\n\n4. Report linguistic quality of translations (BLEU, ChrF against professional translator subset).\n\n---\n\n## 4. Metrics: Additions & Refinements\n\n### 4.1 Add Statistical Significance Testing\n\n- For all main results, report **bootstrap 95% confidence intervals** (1,000 iterations).\n- For pairwise model comparisons, report **paired bootstrap test** p-values.\n- Mark statistically significant differences (p < 0.05) in tables.\n\n### 4.2 Add Tolerance-Based Numeric Accuracy\n\nFollowing FinLongDocQA:\n- **Tol-1**: accuracy within ±1% of gold value.\n- **Tol-5**: accuracy within ±5%.\n- **Tol-10**: accuracy within ±10%.\n\n### 4.3 Add Relaxed Exact Match\n\n- **Relaxed EM**: match after normalizing units (₹ in Crores vs. Lakhs vs. absolute), currency symbols, percentage signs.\n- This is important because Indian reports use varying unit conventions.\n\n### 4.4 Add Attribution / Citation Quality Metrics\n\nFollowing ARQA and FINLFQA:\n- **Citation precision:** Fraction of cited evidence chunks that are actually relevant.\n- **Citation recall:** Fraction of required evidence chunks that are cited.\n- **Citation F1.**\n\n### 4.5 LLM-Judge Calibration\n\n- Calibrate the LLM-judge against human labels on the 500-pair validation set.\n- Report: Spearman correlation between judge scores and human faithfulness ratings.\n- Report: ROC-AUC of judge for binary hallucination detection.\n- Compare multiple judge models: GPT-4o, Claude-3.5-Sonnet, Llama-3.1-70B.\n\n---\n\n## 5. Experimental Design (Revised)\n\n### 5.1 Model Panel (Concrete, Current Models)\n\n**Open-weight models via NVIDIA NIM (or vLLM):**\n\n| Category | Model | Params |\n|----------|-------|--------|\n| Small | Qwen3-8B-Instruct | 8B |\n| Small | Llama-3.2-3B-Instruct | 3B |\n| Small | Gemma-3-4B-Instruct | 4B |\n| Medium | Qwen3-14B-Instruct | 14B |\n| Medium | Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct | 8B |\n| Medium | Mistral-Small-3.2 | 24B |\n| Large | Qwen3-72B-Instruct | 72B |\n| Large | Llama-3.3-70B-Instruct | 70B |\n| Large | DeepSeek-R1-Distill-70B | 70B |\n| Reasoning | DeepSeek-R1-0528 | 671B MoE |\n| Reasoning | Qwen3-235B-A22B | 235B MoE |\n| Indian | Sarvam-105B | 106B MoE |\n\n**Closed models (API):**\n\n| Model | Purpose |\n|-------|---------|\n| GPT-4o | Upper bound reference |\n| GPT-5.2 (if available) | Frontier reference |\n| Claude-3.5-Sonnet | Alternative frontier |\n\n**Financial-specific models:**\n\n| Model | Purpose |\n|-------|---------|\n| FinMA-7B | Domain-specific baseline |\n| FinGPT | Domain-specific baseline |\n\n**Total: 15+ models.** This is comparable to FinBen (21), MULTIFINBEN (21), and BizFinBench (25).\n\n### 5.2 Evaluation Regimes\n\nFor each model:\n\n1. **Zero-shot, open-book (gold context)** — primary result.\n2. **Few-shot (5 examples), open-book (gold context)** — in-context learning effect.\n3. **Zero-shot, closed-book** — knowledge vs. reading comprehension gap.\n4. **Zero-shot, RAG (retrieved context)** — practical pipeline evaluation.\n\n### 5.3 NEW: Cross-Benchmark Comparison\n\nTo show FinBharat's unique challenges, evaluate the same models on:\n- **FinQA** (test set) — numeric reasoning baseline.\n- **TAT-QA** (test set) — table-text hybrid baseline.\n- **PHANTOM** — hallucination detection baseline.\n\nReport: \"Model X achieves Y on FinQA but only Z on FinBharat-Hard, demonstrating that...\"\n\n### 5.4 Ablation Studies (Enhanced)\n\n1. **Open-book vs. Closed-book** (original) ✓\n2. **Zero-shot vs. Few-shot** (original) ✓\n3. **Gold context vs. RAG-retrieved context** (original) ✓\n4. **NEW: Table serialization format:**\n   - HTML table (current) vs. Markdown vs. Linearized key-value vs. Vertical bar format.\n   - This is crucial — table format significantly affects model performance (shown by TAT-QA, FinQA).\n5. **NEW: Context window size:**\n   - Full bundle context vs. truncated to 2k/4k/8k tokens.\n   - Shows impact of document length on performance.\n6. **NEW: Reasoning mode:**\n   - Standard generation vs. Chain-of-Thought vs. Program-of-Thought (code generation for numeric).\n   - Follows FinAgent-RAG's finding that PoT significantly improves numeric accuracy.\n\n### 5.5 Error Analysis (Enhanced)\n\n1. **Scale:** 200 error cases (50 per difficulty tier), up from 100.\n2. **Error taxonomy** (refined):\n   - **Numeric errors:** Calculation error, Unit mismatch, Wrong operand, Scale error (Crores vs. Lakhs).\n   - **Retrieval errors:** Wrong section, Wrong table, Missed footnote.\n   - **Reasoning errors:** Wrong comparison direction, Incomplete multi-hop chain, Speculation beyond evidence.\n   - **Formatting errors:** Non-numeric answer to numeric question, Verbose vs. concise.\n   - **Language errors:** (Hindi subset) Translation artifact, Entity name mismatch.\n\n3. **Quantitative error analysis:**\n   - What fraction of numeric errors are due to unit/scale mismatches (uniquely Indian)?\n   - What fraction of hallucinations are in BRSR sections vs. financial statements?\n   - Correlation between error type and question type / difficulty.\n\n4. **Qualitative examples:** 3–4 detailed case studies showing error → root cause → metric capture.\n\n---\n\n## 6. Paper Structure (Revised for 8+1 Pages)\n\n**Page budget:** 8 content pages + 1 extra for reviewer responses + unlimited appendix.\n\n| Section | Pages | Content |\n|---------|-------|---------|\n| 1. Introduction | 1.5 | Motivation, gap, contributions (5 bullets) |\n| 2. Related Work | 1 | FinQA, TAT-QA, PHANTOM, FAITH, FinLongDocQA, ARQA, FABRIC, BhashaBench-Finance, MULTIFINBEN, FinBen, FinGround |\n| 3. Dataset Construction | 1.5 | Pipeline, statistics, human validation (κ scores), evidence quality audit, BRSR content analysis |\n| 4. Tasks & Metrics | 1.5 | All 9 tasks, formal metric definitions |\n| 5. Experimental Setup | 0.75 | Model panel, splits, regimes, cross-benchmark setup |\n| 6. Results | 2 | QA accuracy tables, faithfulness results, RAG results, BRSR results, Hindi results, cross-benchmark comparison |\n| 7. Analysis | 1 | Error taxonomy, sector-wise analysis, ablation studies, table format analysis |\n| 8. Conclusion & Release | 0.25 | Summary, open-source commitment |\n| Limitations | 0.5 (outside page limit) | Required by EMNLP — desk reject without it |\n| Ethics | 0.25 (outside page limit) | Data licensing, PII, financial advice disclaimer |\n| Appendix | — | Full result tables, prompt templates, annotation guidelines, additional case studies |\n\n### 6.1 Key Tables & Figures\n\n1. **Table 1:** Dataset statistics comparison with existing benchmarks (like the table in §1.3 above).\n2. **Table 2:** Overall QA results across all models × difficulty tiers.\n3. **Table 3:** Faithfulness/hallucination detection results.\n4. **Table 4:** RAG pipeline comparison (5 architectures × metrics).\n5. **Table 5:** BRSR/ESG-specific results.\n6. **Table 6:** English vs. Hindi performance gap.\n7. **Table 7:** Cross-benchmark comparison (FinQA, TAT-QA, PHANTOM vs. FinBharat).\n8. **Table 8:** Ablation studies summary.\n9. **Figure 1:** Dataset construction pipeline.\n10. **Figure 2:** Performance degradation curve across difficulty tiers.\n11. **Figure 3:** Hallucination type distribution by section type.\n12. **Figure 4:** Error taxonomy sunburst chart.\n13. **Figure 5:** RAG retrieval gap (gold vs. retrieved context).\n\n---\n\n## 7. Engineering Roadmap (Revised & Prioritized)\n\n### Phase 1: Data Consolidation & Quality (Weeks 1–3)\n\n1. **Merge all per-company data** into unified indexes with global IDs.\n2. **Create train/dev/test splits** with stratification.\n3. **Run evidence grounding audit** on 1,000 QA pairs.\n4. **Extract BRSR-specific QAs** into separate subset.\n5. **Human validation** of 500 QA pairs (recruit annotators, design guidelines, compute κ).\n\n### Phase 2: Metric Implementation (Weeks 2–4)\n\n1. Numeric parsing + Num-Exact/Num-F1/MAPE + tolerance-based accuracy.\n2. Relaxed EM with Indian unit normalization.\n3. Boolean/directional accuracy.\n4. BERTScore + ROUGE-L.\n5. NLI-based entailment (DeBERTa-v3-large-mnli).\n6. LLM-judge harness (GPT-4o, Claude, Llama-70B).\n7. Evidence traceability + completeness.\n8. Attribution/citation metrics.\n9. **Statistical significance testing** (bootstrap CI, paired tests).\n\n### Phase 3: Model Evaluation (Weeks 3–6)\n\n1. **Core QA evaluation** — all models, all difficulty tiers, gold context.\n2. **Faithfulness evaluation** — NLI + LLM-judge on all model outputs.\n3. **Closed-book evaluation** — subset.\n4. **Few-shot evaluation** — subset.\n5. **RAG pipeline evaluation** — 5 retrieval architectures + 3 generator models.\n6. **BRSR-specific evaluation.**\n7. **Hindi evaluation** — on translated subset.\n8. **Cross-benchmark evaluation** — FinQA, TAT-QA, PHANTOM.\n\n### Phase 4: Analysis & Ablation (Weeks 5–7)\n\n1. **Ablation studies** (table format, context window, reasoning mode).\n2. **Error analysis** (200 cases, taxonomy, quantitative + qualitative).\n3. **Sector-wise and section-wise analysis.**\n4. **LLM-judge calibration** against human labels.\n\n### Phase 5: Paper & Release (Weeks 7–9)\n\n1. Generate all tables and figures.\n2. Write paper sections.\n3. Write Limitations and Ethics sections.\n4. Prepare HuggingFace dataset release.\n5. Prepare GitHub repo with evaluation code, prompts, model configs.\n6. Set up leaderboard (optional but recommended).\n\n---\n\n## 8. NEW: Limitations Section (Required by EMNLP)\n\nThe paper must include a \"Limitations\" section. Plan to address:\n\n1. **Single fiscal year (FY2025):** Temporal generalization is not tested; future work should add multi-year data.\n2. **LLM-generated QA:** Despite human validation of a subset, the majority of QA pairs are model-generated; some noise is inevitable.\n3. **One company per sector:** Limits within-sector variance analysis.\n4. **Hindi translation quality:** Translated subset may have artifacts; professional translation covers only 500 pairs.\n5. **No vision/multimodal evaluation:** Annual reports contain charts and images; our text-only extraction omits these.\n6. **Indian regulatory context:** Findings may not generalize to US 10-K or other regulatory frameworks.\n7. **Retriever-specific results:** RAG results depend on specific retriever choices; other retrievers may yield different outcomes.\n\n---\n\n## 9. NEW: Ethics Statement (Required by EMNLP)\n\n1. **Data licensing:** All annual reports are publicly available from BSE/NSE; no proprietary data.\n2. **PII:** Company names and financial data are public; no individual PII. Verify and strip any accidentally included personal information.\n3. **Financial advice disclaimer:** The benchmark evaluates information extraction and QA, NOT financial advice. Include prominent disclaimer.\n4. **Dual use:** The forensic QA track could be used for both governance improvement and adversarial purposes; we frame it positively.\n5. **Carbon footprint:** Report estimated GPU hours and carbon emissions for all model evaluations.\n6. **Hindi translation consent:** Ensure translators are fairly compensated.\n\n---\n\n## 10. NEW: Release Plan & Leaderboard\n\n### 10.1 HuggingFace Dataset\n\n- Release the **test set** (QA pairs + evidence + metadata) publicly.\n- Release the **dev set** publicly.\n- Keep train set optional (for fine-tuning experiments).\n- Include both English and Hindi subsets.\n- Include the human-validated subset with annotator agreement scores.\n- Data card with: dataset summary, supported tasks, languages, stats, annotation details, licensing.\n\n### 10.2 GitHub Repository\n\n- Evaluation scripts for all metrics.\n- Model inference wrappers (NIM API + vLLM).\n- Prompt templates used in all experiments.\n- Raw model outputs for reproducibility.\n- RAG pipeline implementations.\n- Analysis and visualization notebooks.\n\n### 10.3 Leaderboard (Recommended)\n\n- Set up a simple leaderboard (can be HuggingFace Spaces + Gradio).\n- Categories: FinBharat-QA, FinBharat-Hall, FinBharat-RAG, FinBharat-ESG.\n- Allow submission of model outputs for evaluation on the hidden test set.\n- This is increasingly expected — FinBen, MULTIFINBEN, FABRIC all have leaderboards.\n\n---\n\n## 11. Timeline (Adjusted for ARR May 2026 Cycle)\n\n| Week | Dates | Milestone |\n|------|-------|-----------|\n| 1 | May 25–31 | Data consolidation, split creation, human validation setup |\n| 2 | Jun 1–7 | Metric implementation begins, start human annotation |\n| 3 | Jun 8–14 | Complete human validation, evidence audit, BRSR subset extraction |\n| 4 | Jun 15–21 | Core QA evaluation (all models), metric implementation complete |\n| 5 | Jun 22–28 | Faithfulness evaluation, closed-book + few-shot experiments |\n| 6 | Jun 29–Jul 5 | RAG pipeline evaluation, Hindi evaluation |\n| 7 | Jul 6–12 | Cross-benchmark evaluation, ablation studies |\n| 8 | Jul 13–19 | Error analysis, sector analysis, judge calibration |\n| 9 | Jul 20–26 | Paper writing, figure/table generation |\n| 10 | Jul 27–Aug 2 | Paper revision, release preparation, submission to ARR |\n\n---\n\n## 12. Summary: What Was Changed and Why\n\n| Change | Why |\n|--------|-----|\n| Renamed to FinBharat | Signals India-focus; aligns with naming conventions |\n| Added human validation (500 QA pairs, κ scores) | Biggest gap — reviewers will reject LLM-only QA without validation |\n| Added BRSR/ESG track | Unique differentiator — no other benchmark covers Indian BRSR |\n| Added RAG evaluation track | Every 2025–26 benchmark has this; omission would look outdated |\n| Added bilingual (Hindi) evaluation | FABRIC showed this is compelling; EMNLP values multilinguality |\n| Added cross-benchmark comparison | Reviewers will ask \"how does this compare?\" |\n| Added statistical significance testing | Standard expectation at top venues |\n| Added tolerance-based numeric accuracy | FinLongDocQA set this standard |\n| Added table format ablation | Indian reports have unique table formats; this is a novel finding |\n| Added concrete model names | Original had placeholders; needed for feasibility |\n| Added error taxonomy with Indian-specific categories | Unit/scale mismatches, BRSR-specific errors |\n| Added Limitations section | EMNLP desk-rejects without it |\n| Added Ethics statement | Required by ARR checklist |\n| Added HuggingFace + leaderboard release | Expected for benchmark papers in 2026 |\n| Added attribution/citation metrics | ARQA and FINLFQA demonstrated importance |\n| Added PoT reasoning ablation | FinAgent-RAG showed significant gains |\n| Revised paper structure | Better page allocation, cross-benchmark table |\n\n---\n\n*This updated plan is designed to make FinBharat a strong, competitive submission to EMNLP 2026 that addresses every concern that reviewers raised about recent financial NLP benchmarks, while leveraging the unique strengths of the Indian annual report dataset.*\n"

## Assistant (Build · GLM-5.1 · 340.5s)

Here's my analysis and the updated plan:

## Assessment of Original Plan

**Strengths:** Well-structured task definitions (6 tasks), solid metric suite (Num-Exact, Num-F1, NLI entailment, LLM-judge), good stratification strategy, and a clear engineering roadmap.

**Critical Gaps Identified:**

1. **No human validation** — All 84K+ QA pairs are LLM-generated. EMNLP reviewers will hammer this. PHANTOM, FAITH, ARQA all have human-annotated subsets with κ scores.
2. **No RAG evaluation** — Every competitive 2025–26 financial benchmark (FinLongDocQA, FinAgentBench, FinDoc-RAG, OmniEval) includes a RAG track. Omission looks outdated.
3. **No BRSR/ESG-specific track** — Your dataset is uniquely rich in Indian BRSR content but the plan treats it as generic "section analysis." This is your biggest differentiator over US-focused benchmarks.
4. **No multilingual coverage** — FABRIC and BhashaBench-Finance demonstrate that Indian financial benchmarks *must* include Hindi. EMNLP values multilinguality.
5. **Vague model panel** — "Family A-7B" placeholders; need concrete models (Qwen3, Llama-3.3, DeepSeek-R1, Sarvam, etc.)
6. **No cross-benchmark comparison** — Reviewers will ask "how does this compare to FinQA/PHANTOM/FAITH?"
7. **Missing Limitations + Ethics sections** — EMNLP desk-rejects papers without these.
8. **No statistical significance testing** — Standard at top venues.
9. **No leaderboard/HuggingFace release** — Expected for benchmark papers in 2026.
10. **Missing table format ablation** — Indian reports use unique unit conventions (Crores/Lakhs); this is a novel finding opportunity.

The updated plan is written to **`/home/tanmay/EMNLP2026/finBharat_plan_updated.md`** with all 12 gaps addressed, concrete model names, a 10-week timeline aligned with the ARR May 2026 cycle, and a revised paper structure with page budgets.

## Results: 3 Selected Companies for Initial Prototyping

Based on thorough analysis of 10 candidate companies across 10 sectors, I selected the following 3 from maximally diverse sectors (Financial Services, Manufacturing, and Transportation/Services), prioritizing question_type diversity and dataset size.

---

### 1. HDFC Bank -- Financial Sector

| Field | Value |
|---|---|
| **Sector** | Private_Sector_Bank |
| **Company** | HDFC_Bank |
| **File paths** | Easy: `/home/tanmay/EMNLP2026/dataset/output_final_e_m/Private_Sector_Bank/HDFC_Bank/FY2025/easy/test_easy_qa.jsonl` |
| | Medium: `/home/tanmay/EMNLP2026/dataset/output_final_e_m/Private_Sector_Bank/HDFC_Bank/FY2025/medium/test_medium_qa.jsonl` |
| **Lines in test_easy_qa.jsonl** | **133** |
| **Lines in test_medium_qa.jsonl** | 133 |
| **Easy QA question_type diversity** | 3 types: Text Only (60), Table Only (71), Table with Text (2) |
| **Medium QA question_type diversity** | **All 4 types**: Text Only (26), Table Only (85), Table with Text (12), Numerical Calculation (10) |

**Why selected**: HDFC Bank has the strongest overall question_type diversity across both easy and medium QA. It is the only company where the medium file contains all 4 question types with substantial counts (10 Numerical Calculation, 12 Table with Text). Financial sector data also tends to involve complex tabular reasoning, making it an excellent stress test for prototypes.

---

### 2. Bosch -- Manufacturing Sector

| Field | Value |
|---|---|
| **Sector** | Auto_Components_&_Equipments |
| **Company** | Bosch |
| **File paths** | Easy: `/home/tanmay/EMNLP2026/dataset/output_final_e_m/Auto_Components_&_Equipments/Bosch/FY2025/easy/test_easy_qa.jsonl` |
| | Medium: `/home/tanmay/EMNLP2026/dataset/output_final_e_m/Auto_Components_&_Equipments/Bosch/FY2025/medium/test_medium_qa.jsonl` |
| **Lines in test_easy_qa.jsonl** | **132** |
| **Lines in test_medium_qa.jsonl** | 130 |
| **Easy QA question_type diversity** | 2 types: Text Only (42), Table Only (90) |
| **Medium QA question_type diversity** | **All 4 types**: Text Only (16), Table Only (89), Table with Text (17), Numerical Calculation (8) |

**Why selected**: Bosch represents the manufacturing/auto sector and has excellent question_type diversity in its medium QA file -- all 4 types present, with a notably high count of "Table with Text" (17, the highest among all candidates) and "Numerical Calculation" (8, second only to HDFC Bank). This provides strong coverage of multi-modal reasoning questions.

**Why selected**: Interglobe Aviation represents the services/transportation sector (airlines). **Key observations:**
- All 3 selected companies have **all 4 question types** represented across their easy + medium QA files combined, with all 4 appearing in the medium file alone.
- The 3 sectors are maximally diverse: **Financial** (banking), **Manufacturing** (auto components), and **Services** (airline transportation).
- "Numerical Calculation" questions are relatively rare overall and only appear in the medium difficulty files; HDFC Bank has the most (10), followed by Bosch (8) and Interglobe (3).
- Easy QA files are dominated by "Text Only" and "Table Only" types across all companies in the dataset; the richer question types ("Table with Text", "Numerical Calculation") primarily appear in medium QA.

{
  "content": "import json\nimport re\nfrom dataclasses import dataclass, field\nfrom pathlib import Path\nfrom typing import Optional\n\n\n@dataclass\nclass QARecord:\n    question: str\n    answer: str\n    evidence: str\n    question_type: str\n    requires_calculation: bool\n    sector: str\n    company: str\n    year: str\n    section: str\n    page_numbers: list[int]\n    bundle_id: str\n    difficulty: str\n    global_id: Optional[str] = None\n\n\n@dataclass\nclass ChunkRecord:\n    chunk_id: str\n    doc_id: str\n    sector: str\n    company: str\n    year: str\n    section: str\n    page_numbers: list[int]\n    has_table: bool\n    token_count: int\n    oversized_table: bool\n    score: int\n    text: str\n\n\n@dataclass\nclass BundleRecord:\n    bundle_id: str\n    score: int\n    chunk_count: int\n    section: str\n\n\ndef load_jsonl(path: Path) -> list[dict]:\n    records = []\n    with open(path, \"r\", encoding=\"utf-8\") as f:\n        for line in f:\n            line = line.strip()\n            if line:\n                records.append(json.loads(line))\n    return records\n\n\ndef parse_qa_record(raw: dict, idx: int = 0) -> QARecord:\n    return QARecord(\n        question=raw[\"question\"],\n        answer=raw[\"answer\"],\n        evidence=raw[\"evidence\"],\n        question_type=raw.get(\"question_type\", \"Unknown\"),\n        requires_calculation=raw.get(\"requires_calculation\", False),\n        sector=raw.get(\"sector\", \"\"),\n        company=raw.get(\"company\", \"\"),\n        year=raw.get(\"year\", \"\"),\n        section=raw.get(\"section\", \"\"),\n        page_numbers=raw.get(\"page_numbers\", []),\n        bundle_id=raw.get(\"bundle_id\", \"\"),\n        difficulty=raw.get(\"difficulty\", \"easy\"),\n        global_id=raw.get(\"global_id\", f\"{raw.get('company','unk')}_{idx}\"),\n    )\n\n\ndef parse_chunk_record(raw: dict) -> ChunkRecord:\n    return ChunkRecord(\n        chunk_id=raw[\"chunk_id\"],\n        doc_id=raw[\"doc_id\"],\n        sector=raw.get(\"sector\", \"\"),\n        company=raw.get(\"company\", \"\"),\n        year=raw.get(\"year\", \"\"),\n        section=raw.get(\"section\", \"\"),\n        page_numbers=raw.get(\"page_numbers\", []),\n        has_table=raw.get(\"has_table\", False),\n        token_count=raw.get(\"token_count\", 0),\n        oversized_table=raw.get(\"oversized_table\", False),\n        score=raw.get(\"score\", 0),\n        text=raw[\"text\"],\n    )\n\n\ndef parse_bundle_record(raw: dict) -> BundleRecord:\n    return BundleRecord(\n        bundle_id=raw[\"bundle_id\"],\n        score=raw.get(\"score\", 0),\n        chunk_count=raw.get(\"chunk_count\", 0),\n        section=raw.get(\"section\", \"\"),\n    )\n\n\nSAMPLE_COMPANIES = [\n    {\"sector\": \"Private_Sector_Bank\", \"company\": \"HDFC_Bank\"},\n    {\"sector\": \"Auto_Components_&_Equipments\", \"company\": \"Bosch\"},\n    {\"sector\": \"Airline\", \"company\": \"Interglobe_Aviat\"},\n]\n\n\nclass FinBharatDataset:\n    def __init__(self, data_root: Path):\n        self.data_root = Path(data_root)\n        self.e_m_root = self.data_root / \"output_final_e_m\"\n        self.h_m_root = self.data_root / \"output_final_h_m\"\n\n    def _company_dir(self, sector: str, company: str, source: str = \"e_m\") -> Path:\n        root = self.e_m_root if source == \"e_m\" else self.h_m_root\n        return root / sector / company / \"FY2025\"\n\n    def load_easy_qa(self, sector: str, company: str) -> list[QARecord]:\n        path = self._company_dir(sector, company) / \"easy\" / \"test_easy_qa.jsonl\"\n        raw = load_jsonl(path)\n        return [parse_qa_record(r, idx=i) for i, r in enumerate(raw)]\n\n    def load_medium_qa(self, sector: str, company: str) -> list[QARecord]:\n        path = self._company_dir(sector, company) / \"medium\" / \"test_medium_qa.jsonl\"\n        raw = load_jsonl(path)\n        return [parse_qa_record(r, idx=i) for i, r in enumerate(raw)]\n\n    def load_hard_qa(self, sector: str, company: str) -> list[QARecord]:\n        path = self._company_dir(sector, company, source=\"h_m\") / \"hard\" / \"hard_qa_pairs.jsonl\"\n        raw = load_jsonl(path)\n        return [parse_qa_record(r, idx=i) for i, r in enumerate(raw)]\n\n    def load_multihop_qa(self, sector: str, company: str) -> list[QARecord]:\n        path = self._company_dir(sector, company, source=\"h_m\") / \"multihop\" / \"multihop_qa_pairs.jsonl\"\n        raw = load_jsonl(path)\n        return [parse_qa_record(r, idx=i) for i, r in enumerate(raw)]\n\n    def load_chunks(self, sector: str, company: str, source: str = \"e_m\") -> list[ChunkRecord]:\n        path = self._company_dir(sector, company, source) / \"chunks.jsonl\"\n        raw = load_jsonl(path)\n        return [parse_chunk_record(r) for r in raw]\n\n    def load_bundles(self, sector: str, company: str, source: str = \"e_m\") -> list[BundleRecord]:\n        path = self._company_dir(sector, company, source) / \"semantic_bundles_details.jsonl\"\n        raw = load_jsonl(path)\n        return [parse_bundle_record(r) for r in raw]\n\n    def get_bundle_text(self, sector: str, company: str, bundle_id: str, source: str = \"e_m\") -> str:\n        chunks = self.load_chunks(sector, company, source)\n        match = re.match(r\"(.+):(\\d+)_to_(.+):(\\d+)\", bundle_id)\n        if not match:\n            return \"\"\n        start_id = f\"{match.group(1)}:{match.group(2)}\"\n        end_id = f\"{match.group(3)}:{match.group(4)}\"\n        collecting = False\n        texts = []\n        for chunk in chunks:\n            if chunk.chunk_id == start_id:\n                collecting = True\n            if collecting:\n                texts.append(chunk.text)\n            if chunk.chunk_id == end_id:\n                collecting = False\n                break\n        return \"\\n\\n\".join(texts)\n\n    def load_sample(\n        self,\n        companies: list[dict] | None = None,\n        difficulty: str = \"easy\",\n        max_per_company: int | None = None,\n    ) -> list[QARecord]:\n        if companies is None:\n            companies = SAMPLE_COMPANIES\n\n        loader_map = {\n            \"easy\": self.load_easy_qa,\n            \"medium\": self.load_medium_qa,\n            \"hard\": self.load_hard_qa,\n            \"multihop\": self.load_multihop_qa,\n        }\n        loader = loader_map.get(difficulty)\n        if loader is None:\n            raise ValueError(f\"Unknown difficulty: {difficulty}\")\n\n        all_records: list[QARecord] = []\n        for comp in companies:\n            records = loader(comp[\"sector\"], comp[\"company\"])\n            for i, r in enumerate(records):\n                r.global_id = f\"{comp['company']}_{difficulty}_{i}\"\n            if max_per_company is not None:\n                records = records[:max_per_company]\n            all_records.extend(records)\n        return all_records\n\n    def build_context_for_qa(self, qa: QARecord) -> str:\n        source = \"e_m\" if qa.difficulty in (\"easy\", \"medium\") else \"h_m\"\n        return self.get_bundle_text(qa.sector, qa.company, qa.bundle_id, source)\n\n    def list_available_companies(self, source: str = \"e_m\") -> list[dict]:\n        root = self.e_m_root if source == \"e_m\" else self.h_m_root\n        companies = []\n        for sector_dir in sorted(root.iterdir()):\n            if not   "content": "import re\nfrom dataclasses import dataclass\n\n\n@dataclass\nclass NumericResult:\n    num_exact: int\n    num_f1: float\n    num_precision: float\n    num_recall: float\n 1_000,\n}\n\n_CURRENCY_SYMBOLS = {\"₹\", \"$\", \"€\", \"£\", \"Rs\", \"INR\", \"USD\", \"EUR\"}\n\n_NUMBER_REGEX = re.compile(\n    r\"[₹$€£]?\\s*(?:Rs\\.?\\s*|INR\\s*|USD\\s*)?\"\n    r\"[\\d,]+(?:\\.\\d+)?\"\n    r\"\\s*(?:%|per\\s*cent|percent|crore|cr|lakh|lac|lk|million|mn|billion|bn|thousand|k)?\",\n    re.IGNORECASE,\n)\n\n_PURE_NUMBER_REGEX = _PURE_NUMBER_REGEX.search(m)\n        if pure:\n            numbers.append(pure.group())\n    return numbers\n\n\ndef normalize_number(num_str: str) -> float | None:\n    cleaned = num_str.replace(\",\", \"\").strip()\n    try:\n        return float(cleaned)\n    except ValueError:\n        return None\n\n\ndef normalize_indian_number(num_str: str, unit_hint: str = \"\") -> float | None:\n    base = normalize_number(num_str)\n    if base is None:\n        return None\n    unit_hint_lower = unit_hint.lower().strip()\n    for unit_name, multiplier in _INDIAN_UNIT_MAP.items():\n        if unit_name in unit_hint_lower and multiplier is not None:\n            return base * multiplier\n    return base\n\n\ndef compute_numeric_metrics(gold: str, pred: str) -> NumericResult:\n    gold_nums_raw = extract_numbers(gold)\n    pred_nums_raw = extract_numbers(pred)\n    gold_nums = [normalize_number(n) for n in gold_nums_raw]\n    pred_nums = [normalize_number(n) for n in pred_nums_raw]\n    gold_nums = [n for n in gold_nums if n is not None]\n    pred_nums = [n for n in pred_nums if n is not None]\n\n    if not gold_nums:\n        has_target = False\n        num_exact = 0\n    else:\n        has_target = True\n        target = gold_nums[-1]\n        num_exact = 1 if pred_nums and abs(pred_nums[-1] - target) < 0.01 else 0\n\n    if not gold_nums and not pred_nums:\n        return NumericResult(\n            num_exact=1, num_f1=1.0, num_precision=1.0, num_recall=1.0,\n            gold_numbers=gold_nums_raw, pred_numbers=pred_nums_raw,\n        )\n\n    gold_set = _build_number_set(gold_nums)\n    pred_set = _build_number_set(pred_nums)\n\n    tp = len(gold_set & pred_set)\n    fp = len(pred_set - gold_set)\n    fn = len(gold_set - pred_set)\n\n    precision = tp / (tp + fp) if (tp + fp) > 0 else 0.0\n    recall = tp / (tp + fn) if (tp + fn) > 0 else 0.0\n    f1 = 2 * precision * recall / (precision + recall) if (precision + recall) > 0 else 0.0\n\n    return NumericResult(\n        num_exact=num_exact,\n        num_f1=round(f1, 4),\n        num_precision=round(precision, 4),\n        num_recall=round(recall, 4),\n        gold_numbers=gold_nums_raw,\n        pred_numbers=pred_nums_raw,\n    )\n\n\ndef _build_number_set(nums: list[float], tol: float = 0.01) -> set[int]:\n    rounded = set()\n    for n in nums:\n        bucket = round(n / tol)\n        rounded.add(bucket)\n    return rounded\n\n\ndef compute_tolerance_accuracy(gold: str, pred: str, tolerance_pct: float = 5.0) -> int:\n    gold_nums = [n for n in (normalize_number(x) for x in extract_numbers(gold)) if n is not None]\n    pred_nums = [n for n in (normalize_number(x) for x in extract_numbers(pred)) if n is not None]\n    if not gold_nums or not pred_nums:\n        return 0\n    target = gold_nums[-1]\n    predicted = pred_nums[-1]\n    if abs(target) < 1e-10:\n        return 1 if abs(predicted) < 1e-10 else 0\n    pct_error = abs(predicted - target) / abs(target) * 100\n    return 1 if pct_error <= tolerance_pct else 0\n\n\ndef compute_mape(golds: list[str], preds: list[str]) -> float | None:\n    errors = []\n    for g, p in zip(golds, preds):\n        gold_nums = [n for n in (normalize_number(x) for x in extract_numbers(g)) if n is not None]\n        pred_nums = [n for n in (normalize_number(x) for x in extract_numbers(p)) if n is not None]\n        if gold_nums and pred_nums:\n            target = gold_nums[-1]\n            predicted = pred_nums[-1]\n            if abs(target) > 1e-10:\n                errors.append(abs(predicted - target) / abs(target))\n    if not errors:\n        return None\n    return sum(errors) / len(errors) * 100\n",

float\n\n\n_DIRECTION_MAP = {\n    \"increase\": \"up\", \"increased\": \"up\", \"growth\": \"up\", \"grew\": \"up\",\n    \"rose\": \"up\", \"higher\": \"up\", \"up\": \"up\", \"gain\": \"up\", \"gained\": \"up\",\n    \"decrease\": \"down\", \"decreased\": \"down\", \"decline\": \"down\", \"declined\": \"down\",\n    \"fell\": \"down\", \"drop\": \"down\", \"dropped\": \"down\", \"lower\": \"flat\", \"flat\": \"flat\",\n    \"yes\": \"yes\", \"no\": \"no\",\n}\n\n\ndef normalize_text(text: str) -> str:\n    text = text.lower().strip()\n    text = return 1 if normalize_text(gold) == normalize_text(pred) else 0\n\n\ndef compute_token_f1(gold: str, pred: str) -> TextResult:\n    gold_tokens = set(tokenize(gold))\n    pred_tokens = set(tokenize(pred))\n    if not gold_tokens and not pred_tokens:\n        return TextResult(exact_match=1, f1=1.0, precision=1.0, recall=1.0)\n    if not gold_tokens or not pred_tokens:\n        return TextResult(exact_match=compute_exact_match(gold, pred), f1=0.0, precision=0.0, recall=0.0)\n    common = gold_tokens & pred_tokens\n    tp = len(common)\n    precision = tp / len(pred_tokens)\n    recall = tp / len(gold_tokens)\n    f1 = 2 * precision * recall / (precision + recall) if (precision + recall) > 0 else 0.0\n    return TextResult(\n        exact_match=compute_exact_match(gold, pred),\n        f1=round(f1, 4),\n        precision=round(precision, 4),\n        recall=round(recall, 4),\n    )\n\n\ndef compute_relaxed_em(gold: str, pred: str) -> int:\n    def _strip_units(t:    pf = float(p)\n        return 1 if abs(gf - pf) < 0.015 * max(abs(gf), 1) else 0\n    except ValueError:\n        return 0\n\n\ndef extract_directional_label(text: str) -> str | None:\n    lower = normalize_text(text)\n    words = lower.split()\n    for word in reversed(words):\n        if word in _DIRECTION_MAP:\n            return _DIRECTION_MAP[word]\n    if \"yes\" in lower:\n        return \"yes\"\n    if \"no\" in lower:\n        return \"no\"\n    return None\n\n\ndef compute_directional_accuracy(gold: str, pred: str) -> int | None:\n    gold_label = extract_directional_label(gold)\n    pred_label = extract_directional_label(pred)\n    if gold_label is None:\n        return None\n    if pred_label is None:\n        return 0\n    return 1 if gold_label == pred_label else 0\n",
